</div></div>
<p>The British began their <a href="/wiki/Sinai_and_Palestine_Campaign" class="mw-redirect" title="Sinai and Palestine Campaign">Sinai and Palestine Campaign</a> in 1915.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris200167_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris200167-121">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> The war reached <a href="/wiki/Sinai_and_Palestine_Campaign#Southern_Palestine_Campaign_begins" class="mw-redirect" title="Sinai and Palestine Campaign">southern Palestine in 1917</a>, progressing to Gaza and around <a href="/wiki/Sinai_and_Palestine_Campaign#Jerusalem_Campaign_NovemberâDecember_1917" class="mw-redirect" title="Sinai and Palestine Campaign">Jerusalem by the end of the year</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris200167_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris200167-121">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> The British <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Jerusalem_(1917)" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Jerusalem (1917)">secured Jerusalem in December 1917</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris200167â120_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris200167â120-122">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> They moved into the Jordan valley <a href="/wiki/British_occupation_of_the_Jordan_Valley" title="British occupation of the Jordan Valley">in 1918</a> and a campaign by the Entente into northern Palestine led to victory at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Megiddo_(1918)" title="Battle of Megiddo (1918)">Megiddo in September</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris200167â120_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris200167â120-122">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The British were formally awarded <a href="/wiki/Mandatory_Palestine" title="Mandatory Palestine">the mandate to govern the region</a> in 1922.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESegev2001270â294_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESegev2001270â294-123">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> The non-Jewish Palestinians revolted in <a href="/wiki/1920_Nebi_Musa_riots" title="1920 Nebi Musa riots">1920</a>, <a href="/wiki/1929_Palestine_riots" title="1929 Palestine riots">1929</a>, and <a href="/wiki/1936%E2%80%9339_Arab_revolt_in_Palestine" class="mw-redirect" title="1936â39 Arab revolt in Palestine">1936</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESegev20011â13_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESegev20011â13-124">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> In 1947, following World War II and <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">The Holocaust</a>, the British Government announced its desire to terminate the Mandate, and the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly" title="United Nations General Assembly">United Nations General Assembly</a> adopted in November 1947 a <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Partition_Plan_for_Palestine" title="United Nations Partition Plan for Palestine">Resolution 181(II)</a> recommending partition into an Arab state, a Jewish state and the Special International Regime for the City of Jerusalem.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESegev2001468â487_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESegev2001468â487-125">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/1947%E2%80%9348_Civil_War_in_Mandatory_Palestine" class="mw-redirect" title="1947â48 Civil War in Mandatory Palestine">civil war</a> began immediately after the Resolution's adoption. The <a href="/wiki/State_of_Israel" class="mw-redirect" title="State of Israel">State of Israel</a> was <a href="/wiki/Israeli_Declaration_of_Independence" title="Israeli Declaration of Independence">declared</a> in May 1948.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESegev2001487â521_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESegev2001487â521-126">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-1948">Post-1948</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Israel" title="History of Israel">History of Israel</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_State_of_Palestine" title="History of the State of Palestine">History of the State of Palestine</a></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/1948_Arab%E2%80%93Israeli_War" title="1948 ArabâIsraeli War">1948 ArabâIsraeli War</a>, Israel captured and incorporated a further 26% of the Mandate territory, <a href="/wiki/Jordanian_annexation_of_the_West_Bank" title="Jordanian annexation of the West Bank">Jordan captured</a> the regions of <a href="/wiki/Judea" title="Judea">Judea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Samaria" title="Samaria">Samaria</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPappÃ©1994119_&quot;His_(Abdallah)_natural_choice_was_the_regions_of_Judea_and_Samaria...&quot;_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPappÃ©1994119_&quot;His_(Abdallah)_natural_choice_was_the_regions_of_Judea_and_Samaria...&quot;-127">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;xix&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGerson201293_&quot;Trans-Jordan_was_also_in_control_of_all_of_Judea_and_Samaria_(the_West_Bank)&quot;_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGerson201293_&quot;Trans-Jordan_was_also_in_control_of_all_of_Judea_and_Samaria_(the_West_Bank)&quot;-129">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> renaming it the "<a href="/wiki/West_Bank" title="West Bank">West Bank</a>", while the <a href="/wiki/Gaza_Strip" title="Gaza Strip">Gaza Strip</a> was <a href="/wiki/Occupation_of_the_Gaza_Strip_by_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="Occupation of the Gaza Strip by Egypt">captured by Egypt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPappÃ©1994102â135_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPappÃ©1994102â135-130">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKhalidi200712â36_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKhalidi200712â36-131">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Following the <a href="/wiki/1948_Palestinian_expulsion_and_flight" title="1948 Palestinian expulsion and flight">1948 Palestinian expulsion and flight</a>, also known as <a href="/wiki/Nakba" title="Nakba">al-Nakba</a>, the 700,000 Palestinians who fled or were driven from their homes were <a href="/wiki/Prevention_of_Infiltration_Law" title="Prevention of Infiltration Law">not allowed to return</a> following the <a href="/wiki/Lausanne_Conference_of_1949" title="Lausanne Conference of 1949">Lausanne Conference of 1949</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPappÃ©199487â101_and_203â243_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPappÃ©199487â101_and_203â243-132">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the course of the <a href="/wiki/Six-Day_War" title="Six-Day War">Six-Day War</a> in June 1967, Israel captured the rest of Mandate Palestine from Jordan and Egypt, and began a policy of establishing <a href="/wiki/Israeli_settlement" title="Israeli settlement">Jewish settlements</a> in those <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_territories" title="Palestinian territories">territories</a>. From 1987 to 1993, the <a href="/wiki/First_Intifada" title="First Intifada">First Palestinian Intifada</a> against Israel took place, which included the <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_the_State_of_Palestine" class="mw-redirect" title="Declaration of the State of Palestine">Declaration of the State of Palestine</a> in 1988 and ended with the <a href="/wiki/1993_Oslo_Peace_Accords" class="mw-redirect" title="1993 Oslo Peace Accords">1993 Oslo Peace Accords</a> and the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_National_Authority" title="Palestinian National Authority">Palestinian National Authority</a>.
</p><p>In 2000, the <a href="/wiki/Second_Intifada" title="Second Intifada">Second Intifada</a> (also called al-Aqsa Intifada) began, and Israel built a <a href="/wiki/Israeli_West_Bank_barrier" title="Israeli West Bank barrier">separation barrier</a>. In the 2005 <a href="/wiki/Israeli_disengagement_from_Gaza" title="Israeli disengagement from Gaza">Israeli disengagement from Gaza</a>, Israel withdrew all settlers and military presence from the Gaza Strip, but maintained military control of numerous aspects of the territory including its borders, air space and coast. Israel's ongoing military occupation of the Gaza Strip, the West Bank and East Jerusalem continues to be the world's <a href="/wiki/List_of_military_occupations" title="List of military occupations">longest military occupation</a> in modern times.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;xx&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;xxi&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In November 2012, the status of Palestinian delegation in the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> was upgraded to <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly_observers#Non-member_observers" title="United Nations General Assembly observers">non-member observer state</a> as the <a href="/wiki/State_of_Palestine" title="State of Palestine">State of Palestine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUN_GA/113172012_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUN_GA/113172012-145">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;xxii&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Boundaries">Boundaries</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-modern">Pre-modern</span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Satellite_image_of_Israel.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Satellite_image_of_Israel.jpg/110px-Satellite_image_of_Israel.jpg" decoding="async" width="110" height="282" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Satellite_image_of_Israel.jpg/165px-Satellite_image_of_Israel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Satellite_image_of_Israel.jpg/220px-Satellite_image_of_Israel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="677" data-file-height="1738" /></a><figcaption>Satellite image of the region</figcaption></figure>
<p>The boundaries of Palestine have varied throughout history.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;xxiii&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;xxiv&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Jordan_Rift_Valley" title="Jordan Rift Valley">Jordan Rift Valley</a> (comprising Wadi Arabah, the <a href="/wiki/Dead_Sea" title="Dead Sea">Dead Sea</a> and <a href="/wiki/River_Jordan" class="mw-redirect" title="River Jordan">River Jordan</a>) has at times formed a political and administrative frontier, even within empires that have controlled both territories.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAharoni197964_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAharoni197964-151">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> At other times, such as during certain periods during the <a href="/wiki/Hasmonean" class="mw-redirect" title="Hasmonean">Hasmonean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Jerusalem" title="Kingdom of Jerusalem">Crusader</a> states for example, as well as during the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Israel_(united_monarchy)" title="Kingdom of Israel (united monarchy)">biblical period</a>, territories on both sides of the river formed part of the same administrative unit. During the <a href="/wiki/Arab_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Arab people">Arab</a> <a href="/wiki/Caliphate" title="Caliphate">Caliphate</a> period, parts of southern <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a> and the northern highland areas of Palestine and Jordan were administered as <i><a href="/wiki/Jund_al-Urdunn" title="Jund al-Urdunn">Jund al-Urdun</a></i>, while the southern parts of the latter two formed part of <i><a href="/wiki/Jund_Dimashq" title="Jund Dimashq">Jund Dimashq</a></i>, which during the 9th century was attached to the administrative unit of <i><a href="/wiki/Jund_Filastin" title="Jund Filastin">Jund Filastin</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESalibi199317â18_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESalibi199317â18-152">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The boundaries of the area and the ethnic nature of the people referred to by <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> in the 5th century BCE as Palaestina vary according to context. Sometimes, he uses it to refer to the coast north of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Carmel" title="Mount Carmel">Mount Carmel</a>. Elsewhere, distinguishing the Syrians in Palestine from the Phoenicians, he refers to their land as extending down all the coast from Phoenicia to Egypt.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerodotus1858Bk_vii,_Ch_89_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerodotus1858Bk_vii,_Ch_89-153">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny</a>, writing in <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a> in the 1st century CE, describes a region of Syria that was "formerly called <i>Palaestina</i>" among the areas of the Eastern Mediterranean.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the Byzantine Period, the Byzantine borders of <i>Palaestina</i> (<i>I</i> and <i>II</i>, also known as <i>Palaestina Prima</i>, "First Palestine", and <i>Palaestina Secunda</i>, "Second Palestine"), have served as a name for the geographic area between the Jordan River and the Mediterranean Sea. Under Arab rule, <i>Filastin</i> (or <i>Jund Filastin</i>) was used administratively to refer to what was under the Byzantines <i>Palaestina Secunda</i> (comprising <a href="/wiki/Judaea_and_Samaria" class="mw-redirect" title="Judaea and Samaria">Judaea and Samaria</a>), while <i>Palaestina Prima</i> (comprising the <a href="/wiki/Galilee" title="Galilee">Galilee</a> region) was renamed <i>Urdunn</i> ("Jordan" or <i>Jund al-Urdunn</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharon19884_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharon19884-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_period">Modern period</span></h3>
<p>Nineteenth-century sources refer to Palestine as extending from the sea to the caravan route, presumably the <a href="/wiki/Hejaz_Railway" class="mw-redirect" title="Hejaz Railway">Hejaz-Damascus route</a> east of the Jordan River valley.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBiger200419â20_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBiger200419â20-155">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Others refer to it as extending from the sea to the desert.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBiger200419â20_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBiger200419â20-155">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Prior to the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_I" title="Allies of World War I">Allied Powers</a> victory in World War I and the <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Partition of the Ottoman Empire">partitioning of the Ottoman Empire</a>, which created the British mandate in the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, most of the northern area of what is today Jordan formed part of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> <a href="/wiki/Vilayet_of_Damascus" class="mw-redirect" title="Vilayet of Damascus">Vilayet of Damascus</a> (<a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>), while the southern part of Jordan was part of the <a href="/wiki/Vilayet_of_Hejaz" class="mw-redirect" title="Vilayet of Hejaz">Vilayet of Hejaz</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBiger200413_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBiger200413-156">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> What later became <a href="/wiki/Mandatory_Palestine" title="Mandatory Palestine">Mandatory Palestine</a> was in late Ottoman times divided between the <a href="/wiki/Vilayet_of_Beirut" class="mw-redirect" title="Vilayet of Beirut">Vilayet of Beirut</a> (<a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Sanjak_of_Jerusalem" class="mw-redirect" title="Sanjak of Jerusalem">Sanjak of Jerusalem</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Risalesi_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Risalesi-35">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/World_Zionist_Organization" title="World Zionist Organization">Zionist Organization</a> provided its definition of the boundaries of Palestine in a statement to the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Peace_Conference,_1919" class="mw-redirect" title="Paris Peace Conference, 1919">Paris Peace Conference in 1919</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETessler1994163_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETessler1994163-157">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBiger200441â80_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBiger200441â80-158">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The British administered <a href="/wiki/Mandatory_Palestine" title="Mandatory Palestine">Mandatory Palestine</a> after World War I, having promised to establish a <a href="/wiki/Homeland_for_the_Jewish_people" title="Homeland for the Jewish people">homeland for the Jewish people</a>. The modern definition of the region follows the boundaries of that entity, which were fixed in the North and East in 1920â23 by the <a href="/wiki/Mandate_for_Palestine" title="Mandate for Palestine">British Mandate for Palestine</a> (including the <a href="/wiki/Transjordan_memorandum" class="mw-redirect" title="Transjordan memorandum">Transjordan memorandum</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Paulet%E2%80%93Newcombe_Agreement" title="PauletâNewcombe Agreement">PauletâNewcombe Agreement</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBiger2004133,_159_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBiger2004133,_159-37">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> and on the South by following the 1906 Turco-Egyptian boundary agreement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBiger200480_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBiger200480-159">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKliot19959_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKliot19959-160">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

href="/wiki/File:Proposals_for_the_Mandate_of_Palestine_1916-19.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Proposals_for_the_Mandate_of_Palestine_1916-19.svg/258px-Proposals_for_the_Mandate_of_Palestine_1916-19.svg.png" decoding="async" width="258" height="479" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Proposals_for_the_Mandate_of_Palestine_1916-19.svg/387px-Proposals_for_the_Mandate_of_Palestine_1916-19.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Proposals_for_the_Mandate_of_Palestine_1916-19.svg/516px-Proposals_for_the_Mandate_of_Palestine_1916-19.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1233" data-file-height="2291" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption"><b>1916â1922 various proposals</b>: <span style="font-size:85%;">Three proposals for the post World War I administration of Palestine. The red line is the "International Administration" proposed in the 1916 <a href="/wiki/Sykes%E2%80%93Picot_Agreement" title="SykesâPicot Agreement">SykesâPicot Agreement</a>, the dashed blue line is the 1919 <a href="/wiki/World_Zionist_Organization" title="World Zionist Organization">Zionist Organization</a> proposal at the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Peace_Conference_(1919%E2%80%931920)" title="Paris Peace Conference (1919â1920)">Paris Peace Conference</a>, and the thin blue line refers to the final borders of the 1923â48 <a href="/wiki/Mandatory_Palestine" title="Mandatory Palestine">Mandatory Palestine</a>.</span></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:260px;max-width:260px"><div class="thumbimage"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Peel_map_1937_Palestine.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Peel_map_1937_Palestine.svg/258px-Peel_map_1937_Palestine.svg.png" decoding="async" width="258" height="479" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Peel_map_1937_Palestine.svg/387px-Peel_map_1937_Palestine.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Peel_map_1937_Palestine.svg/516px-Peel_map_1937_Palestine.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1233" data-file-height="2291" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption"><b>1937 British proposal</b>: <span style="font-size:85%;">The first official proposal for partition, published in 1937 by the <a href="/wiki/Peel_Commission" title="Peel Commission">Peel Commission</a>. An ongoing British Mandate was proposed to keep "the sanctity of <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bethlehem" title="Bethlehem">Bethlehem</a>", in the form of an enclave from Jerusalem to <a href="/wiki/Jaffa" title="Jaffa">Jaffa</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Lydda" class="mw-redirect" title="Lydda">Lydda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ramle" class="mw-redirect" title="Ramle">Ramle</a>.</span></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:260px;max-width:260px"><div class="thumbimage"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:United_Nations_Partition_Plan_for_Palestine_map.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/United_Nations_Partition_Plan_for_Palestine_map.svg/258px-United_Nations_Partition_Plan_for_Palestine_map.svg.png" decoding="async" width="258" height="479" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/United_Nations_Partition_Plan_for_Palestine_map.svg/387px-United_Nations_Partition_Plan_for_Palestine_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/United_Nations_Partition_Plan_for_Palestine_map.svg/516px-United_Nations_Partition_Plan_for_Palestine_map.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1233" data-file-height="2291" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption"><b>1947 UN proposal</b>: <span style="font-size:85%;">Proposal per the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Partition_Plan_for_Palestine" title="United Nations Partition Plan for Palestine">United Nations Partition Plan for Palestine</a> (UN General Assembly Resolution 181 (II), 1947), prior to the <a href="/wiki/1948_Arab%E2%80%93Israeli_War" title="1948 ArabâIsraeli War">1948 ArabâIsraeli War</a>. The proposal included a <a href="/wiki/Corpus_separatum_(Jerusalem)" title="Corpus separatum (Jerusalem)">Corpus Separatum for Jerusalem</a>, extraterritorial crossroads between the non-contiguous areas, and <a href="/wiki/Jaffa" title="Jaffa">Jaffa</a> as an Arab exclave.</span></div></div></div></div></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti"><div class="thumb tmulti tnone center"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:786px;max-width:786px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:260px;max-width:260px"><div class="thumbimage"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Jewish_and_Arab_Land_Ownership_in_Mandatory_Palestine,_1947.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Jewish_and_Arab_Land_Ownership_in_Mandatory_Palestine%2C_1947.svg/258px-Jewish_and_Arab_Land_Ownership_in_Mandatory_Palestine%2C_1947.svg.png" decoding="async" width="258" height="479" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Jewish_and_Arab_Land_Ownership_in_Mandatory_Palestine%2C_1947.svg/387px-Jewish_and_Arab_Land_Ownership_in_Mandatory_Palestine%2C_1947.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Jewish_and_Arab_Land_Ownership_in_Mandatory_Palestine%2C_1947.svg/516px-Jewish_and_Arab_Land_Ownership_in_Mandatory_Palestine%2C_1947.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1233" data-file-height="2291" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption"><b>1947 Jewish private land ownership</b>: <span style="font-size:85%;"><a href="/wiki/Jewish_land_purchase_in_Palestine" title="Jewish land purchase in Palestine">Jewish-owned lands in Mandatory Palestine</a> as of 1947 in blue, constituting 7.4% of the total land area, of which more than half was held by the <a href="/wiki/Jewish_National_Fund" title="Jewish National Fund">JNF</a> and <a href="/wiki/Palestine_Jewish_Colonization_Association" title="Palestine Jewish Colonization Association">PICA</a>. White is either <a href="/wiki/Public_land" title="Public land">public land</a> or <a href="/wiki/Palestinian-Arab" class="mw-redirect" title="Palestinian-Arab">Palestinian-Arab</a>-owned lands including related religious trusts.</span></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:260px;max-width:260px"><div class="thumbimage"><span typeof="mw:File"><a style="width:260px;max-width:260px"><div class="thumbimage"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Palestinian_National_Authority_within_Israel,1967-94.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" (later traded for peace after the <a href="/wiki/Yom_Kippur_War" title="Yom Kippur War">Yom Kippur War</a>). In 1980â81 Israel <a href="/wiki/Positions_on_Jerusalem" class="mw-redirect" title="Positions on Jerusalem">annexed East Jerusalem</a> and <a href="/wiki/Golan_Heights_Law" title="Golan Heights Law">the Golan Heights</a>.
Neither Israel's annexation nor the <a href="/wiki/Palestine_Liberation_Organization" title="Palestine Liberation Organization">PLO</a> claim over East Jerusalem gained international recognition.</span></div></div></div></div></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti"><div class="thumb tmulti tnone center"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:709px;max-width:709px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:260px;max-width:260px"><div class="thumbimage"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Palestinian_National_Authority_within_Israel,_1994-2006.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Palestinian_National_Authority_within_Israel%2C_1994-2006.svg/258px-Palestinian_National_Authority_within_Israel%2C_1994-2006.svg.png" decoding="async" width="258" height="479" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Palestinian_National_Authority_within_Israel%2C_1994-2006.svg/387px-Palestinian_National_Authority_within_Israel%2C_1994-2006.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Palestinian_National_Authority_within_Israel%2C_1994-2006.svg/516px-Palestinian_National_Authority_within_Israel%2C_1994-2006.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1233" data-file-height="2291" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption"><b>1995 <a href="/wiki/Oslo_II_Accord" title="Oslo II Accord">Oslo II Accord</a></b>: <span style="font-size:85%;">Under the <a href="/wiki/Oslo_Accords" title="Oslo Accords">Oslo Accords</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_National_Authority" title="Palestinian National Authority">Palestinian National Authority</a> was created to provide a Palestinian interim self-government in the West Bank and the interior of the Gaza Strip. Its second phase envisioned "<a href="/wiki/Palestinian_enclaves" title="Palestinian enclaves">Palestinian enclaves</a>".</span></div></div><div class="tsingle" data-file-width="1233" data-file-height="2291" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption"><b>2005âpresent</b>: <span style="font-size:85%;">After the <a href="/wiki/Israeli_disengagement_from_Gaza" title="Israeli disengagement from Gaza">Israeli disengagement from Gaza</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fatah%E2%80%93Hamas_conflict" title="FatahâHamas conflict">clashes between the two main Palestinian parties</a> following the <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_legislative_election,_2006" class="mw-redirect" title="Palestinian legislative election, 2006">Hamas electoral victory</a>, two separate executive governments took control in the <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_territories" title="Palestinian territories">Palestinian territories</a> of the West Bank and Gaza.</span></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:183px;max-width:183px"><div class="thumbimage"><span typeof="mw:File"><a <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_usage">Current usage</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_territories" title="Palestinian territories">Palestinian territories</a>, <a href="/wiki/State_of_Palestine" title="State of Palestine">State of Palestine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_National_Authority" title="Palestinian National Authority">Palestinian National Authority</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_enclaves" title="Palestinian enclaves">Palestinian enclaves</a></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Borders_of_Israel" title="Borders of Israel">Borders of Israel</a></div>
<p>The region of Palestine is the <a href="/wiki/Eponym" title="Eponym">eponym</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Palestinian people">Palestinian people</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Palestine" title="Culture of Palestine">culture of Palestine</a>, both of which are defined as relating to the whole historical region, usually defined as the localities within the border of <a href="/wiki/Mandatory_Palestine" title="Mandatory Palestine">Mandatory Palestine</a>. The 1968 <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_National_Covenant" title="Palestinian National Covenant">Palestinian National Covenant</a> described Palestine as the "homeland of the Arab Palestinian people", with "the boundaries it had during the British Mandate".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESaidHitchens2001199_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESaidHitchens2001199-161">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>However, since the 1988 <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_Declaration_of_Independence" title="Palestinian Declaration of Independence">Palestinian Declaration of Independence</a>, the term <a href="/wiki/State_of_Palestine" title="State of Palestine">State of Palestine</a> refers only to the West Bank and the Gaza Strip. This discrepancy was described by the Palestinian president Mahmoud Abbas as a negotiated concession in a September 2011 speech to the United Nations: "... we agreed to establish the State of Palestine on only 22% of the territory of historical Palestine â on all the Palestinian Territory occupied by Israel in 1967."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Haaretz&#39;&#39;2011_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;Haaretz&#39;&#39;2011-162">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The term <i>Palestine</i> is also sometimes used in a limited sense to refer to <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_enclaves" title="Palestinian enclaves">the parts of the Palestinian territories currently under the administrative control</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_National_Authority" title="Palestinian National Authority">Palestinian National Authority</a>, a quasi-governmental entity which governs <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_enclaves" title="Palestinian enclaves">parts of the State of Palestine</a> under the terms of the <a href="/wiki/Oslo_Accords" title="Oslo Accords">Oslo Accords</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;xxvi&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<p>Estimating the population of Palestine in antiquity relies on two methods â censuses and writings made at the times, and the scientific method based on excavations and statistical methods that consider the number of settlements at the particular age, area of each settlement, density factor for each settlement.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Bar_Kokhba_revolt" title="Bar Kokhba revolt">Bar Kokhba revolt</a> in the 2nd century CE saw a major shift in the population of Palestine. The sheer scale and scope of the overall destruction has been described by <a href="/wiki/Dio_Cassius" class="mw-redirect" title="Dio Cassius">Dio Cassius</a> in his <i>Roman History</i>, where he notes that Roman war operations in the country had left some 580,000 Jews dead, with many more dying of hunger and disease, while 50 of their most important outposts and 985 of their most famous villages were razed to the ground. "Thus," writes Dio Cassius, "nearly the whole of <a href="/wiki/Judea_(Roman_province)" class="mw-redirect" title="Judea (Roman province)">Judaea</a> was made desolate."<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2012_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2012-167">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Israeli_archaeology" class="mw-redirect" title="Israeli archaeology">Israeli archaeologists</a> Magen Broshi and Yigal Shiloh, the population of ancient Palestine did not exceed one million.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;xxvii&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;xxviii&#93;</a></sup> By 300&#160;CE, Christianity had spread so significantly that Jews comprised only a quarter of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;xxix&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_Ottoman_and_British_Mandate_periods">Late Ottoman and British Mandate periods</span></h3><p>
In a study of <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> registers of the early Ottoman rule of Palestine, <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Lewis" title="Bernard Lewis">Bernard Lewis</a> reports:</p><blockquote><p>[T]he first half century of Ottoman rule brought a sharp increase in population. The towns grew rapidly, villages became larger and more numerous, and there was an extensive development of agriculture, industry, and trade. The two last were certainly helped to no small extent by the influx of Spanish and other Western Jews.</p></blockquote><blockquote><p>From the mass of detail in the registers, it is possible to extract something like a general picture of the economic life of the country in that period. Out of a total population of about 300,000 souls, between a fifth and a quarter lived in the six towns of <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gaza_City" title="Gaza City">Gaza</a>, <a href="/wiki/Safed" title="Safed">Safed</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nablus" title="Nablus">Nablus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ramla" title="Ramla">Ramle</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hebron" title="Hebron">Hebron</a>. The remainder consisted mainly of peasants, living in villages of varying size, and engaged in agriculture. Their main food-crops were wheat and barley in that order, supplemented by leguminous pulses, olives, fruit, and vegetables. In and around most of the towns there was a considerable number of vineyards, orchards, and vegetable gardens.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewis1954487_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewis1954487-171">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>

<td colspan="7" style="font-size:70%; text-align:left;">Historical population table compiled by <a href="/wiki/Sergio_DellaPergola" class="mw-redirect" title="Sergio DellaPergola">Sergio DellaPergola</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDellaPergola20015_165-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDellaPergola20015-165">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> Figures in thousands.
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>According to Alexander Scholch, the population of Palestine in 1850 was about 350,000 inhabitants, 30% of whom lived in 13 towns; roughly 85% were Muslims, 11% were Christians and 4% Jews.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScholch1985503_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScholch1985503-172">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Ottoman statistics studied by <a href="/wiki/Justin_McCarthy_(American_historian)" title="Justin McCarthy (American historian)">Justin McCarthy</a>, the population of Palestine in the early 19th century was 350,000, in 1860 it was 411,000 and in 1900 about 600,000 of whom 94% were <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCarthy199026_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCarthy199026-173">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> In 1914 Palestine had a population of 657,000 Muslim Arabs, 81,000 Christian Arabs, and 59,000 Jews.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCarthy199030_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCarthy199030-174">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> McCarthy estimates the non-Jewish population of Palestine at 452,789 in 1882; 737,389 in 1914; 725,507 in 1922; 880,746 in 1931; and 1,339,763 in 1946.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCarthy199037â38_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCarthy199037â38-175">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>
In 1920, the League of Nations' <i>Interim Report on the Civil Administration of Palestine</i> described the 700,000 people living in Palestine as follows:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKirk201146_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKirk201146-176">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Of these, 235,000 live in the larger towns, 465,000 in the smaller towns and villages. Four-fifths of the whole population are Moslems. A small proportion of these are Bedouin Arabs; the remainder, although they speak Arabic and are termed Arabs, are largely of mixed race. Some 77,000 of the population are Christians, in large majority belonging to the Orthodox Church, and speaking Arabic. The minority are members of the Latin or of the Uniate Greek Catholic Church, orâa small numberâare Protestants.
The Jewish element of the population numbers 76,000. Almost all have entered Palestine during the last 40 years. Prior to 1850, there were in the country only a handful of Jews. In the following 30 years, a few hundreds came to Palestine. Most of them were animated by religious motives; they came to pray and to die in the Holy Land, and to be buried in its soil. After the persecutions in Russia forty years ago, the movement of the Jews to Palestine assumed larger proportions.</p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_demographics">Current demographics</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Israel" title="Demographics of Israel">Demographics of Israel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_the_Palestinian_territories" class="mw-redirect" title="Demographics of the Palestinian territories">Demographics of the Palestinian territories</a></div>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Israel_Central_Bureau_of_Statistics" title="Israel Central Bureau of Statistics">Israel Central Bureau of Statistics</a>, as of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Palestine_(region)&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, the total population of Israel was 8.5&#160;million people, of which 75% were <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a>, 21% <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a>, and 4% "others."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEICBoS:_Population2016_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEICBoS:_Population2016-177">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> Of the Jewish group, 76% were <a href="/wiki/Sabra_(person)" title="Sabra (person)">Sabras</a> (born in Israel); the rest were <a href="/wiki/Aliyah" title="Aliyah">olim</a> (immigrants)â16% from Europe, the former Soviet republics, and the Americas, and 8% from Asia and Africa, including the <a href="/wiki/Arab_world" title="Arab world">Arab countries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEICBoS:_Jews2016_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEICBoS:_Jews2016-178">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_Central_Bureau_of_Statistics" title="Palestinian Central Bureau of Statistics">Palestinian Central Bureau of Statistics</a> evaluations, in 2015 the Palestinian population of the <a href="/wiki/West_Bank" title="West Bank">West Bank</a> was approximately 2.9&#160;million and that of the <a href="/wiki/Gaza_Strip" title="Gaza Strip">Gaza Strip</a> was 1.8&#160;million.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPCBoS:_Estd_Population2016_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPCBoS:_Estd_Population2016-179">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> Gaza's population is expected to increase to 2.1&#160;million people in 2020, leading to a density of more than 5,800 people per square kilometre.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUN_News_Centre2012_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUN_News_Centre2012-180">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Both Israeli and Palestinian statistics include Arab residents of <a href="/wiki/East_Jerusalem" title="East Jerusalem">East Jerusalem</a> in their reports.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMezzofiore2015_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMezzofiore2015-181">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (May 2022)">better&#160;source&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> According to these estimates the total population in the region of Palestine, as defined as Israel and the Palestinian territories, stands approximately 12.8&#160;million.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Flora_and_fauna">Flora and fauna</span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_in_Israel_and_Palestine" title="Biodiversity in Israel and Palestine">Biodiversity in Israel and Palestine</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flora_distribution">Flora distribution</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Flora_of_Palestine_(region)" title="Category:Flora of Palestine (region)">Category:Flora of Palestine (region)</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_native_plants_of_Flora_Palaestina_(A%E2%80%93B)" title="List of native plants of Flora Palaestina (AâB)">List of native plants of Flora Palaestina (AâB)</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/World_Geographical_Scheme_for_Recording_Plant_Distributions" title="World Geographical Scheme for Recording Plant Distributions">World Geographical Scheme for Recording Plant Distributions</a> is widely used in recording the distribution of plants. The scheme uses the code "PAL" to refer to the region of Palestine â a Level 3 area. The WGSRPD's Palestine is further divided into Israel (PAL-IS), including the Palestinian territories, and Jordan (PAL-JO), so is larger than some other definitions of "Palestine".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrummitt2001_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrummitt2001-182">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The earliest occurrence of this name in a Greek text is in the mid-fifth century B.C., Histories of Herodotus, where it is applied to the area of the Levant between Phoenicia and Egypt." ...&#160;"The first known occurrence of the Greek word Palaistine is in the Histories of Herodotus, written near the mid-fifth century B.C. Palaistine Syria, or simply Palaistine, is applied to what may be identified as the southern part of Syria, comprising the region between Phoenicia and Egypt. Although some of Herodotus' references to Palestine are compatible with a narrow definition of the coastal strip of the Land of Israel, it is clear that Herodotus does call the whole land by the name of the coastal strip." ...&#160;"It is believed that Herodotus visited Palestine in the fifth decade of the fifth century B.C." &#160;..."In the earliest Classical literature references to Palestine generally applied to the Land of Israel in the wider sense." (<a href="#CITEREFJacobson1999">Jacobson 1999</a>)</span>

<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"As early as the Histories of Herodotus, written in the second half of the fifth century BCE, the term PalaistinÃª is used to describe not just the geographical area where the Philistines lived, but the entire area between Phoenicia and Egyptâin other words, the Land of Israel. Herodotus, who had traveled through the area, would have had firsthand knowledge of the land and its people. Yet he used PalaistinÃª to refer not to the Land of the Philistines, but to the Land of Israel" (<a href="#CITEREFJacobson2001">Jacobson 2001</a>)</span>

<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In <i><a href="/wiki/Histories_(Herodotus)" title="Histories (Herodotus)">The Histories</a></i>, Herodotus referred to the practice of <a href="/wiki/History_of_male_circumcision" class="mw-redirect" title="History of male circumcision">male circumcision</a> associated with the Hebrew people: "the <a href="/wiki/Colchis" title="Colchis">Colchians</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Egyptians" title="Egyptians">Egyptians</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Ethiopians" title="Ethiopians">Ethiopians</a>, are the only nations who have practised circumcision from the earliest times. The <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicians</a> and the Syrians of Palestine themselves confess that they learnt the custom of the Egyptians&#160;... <li id="cite_note-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-29">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Rabbinic sources insist that the Philistines of Judges and Samuel were different people altogether from the Philistines of Genesis. (<a href="/wiki/Midrash_Tehillim" title="Midrash Tehillim">Midrash Tehillim</a> on Psalm 60 (Braude: vol. 1, 513); the issue here is precisely whether Israel should have been obliged, later, to keep the Genesis treaty.) This parallels a shift in the Septuagint's translation of Hebrew pelistim. Before Judges, it uses the neutral transliteration phulistiim, but beginning with Judges it switches to the pejorative allophuloi. [To be precise, Codex Alexandrinus starts using the new translation at the beginning of Judges and uses it invariably thereafter, Vaticanus likewise switches at the beginning of Judges, but reverts to phulistiim on six occasions later in Judges, the last of which is 14:2.]" (<a href="#CITEREFJoblingRose1996">Jobling &amp; Rose 1996</a>, p.&#160;404)</span>

<li id="cite_note-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-36">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For example, the 1915 <i>Filastin Risalesi</i> ("Palestine Document"), an Ottoman army (<a href="/wiki/VIII_Corps_(Ottoman_Empire)" title="VIII Corps (Ottoman Empire)">VIII Corps</a>) country survey which formally identified Palestine as including the sanjaqs of <a href="/wiki/Acre_Sanjak" title="Acre Sanjak">Akka (the Galilee)</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Nablus_Sanjak" title="Nablus Sanjak">Sanjaq of Nablus</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Mutasarrifate_of_Jerusalem" title="Mutasarrifate of Jerusalem">Sanjaq of Jerusalem (Kudus Sherif)</a><sup id="cite_ref-Risalesi_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Risalesi-35">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup></span>

<li id="cite_note-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-41">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a>, taking up a term used once in the <a href="/wiki/Tanakh" class="mw-redirect" title="Tanakh">Tanakh</a> (1 Samuel 13:19),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhitelam199640â42_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhitelam199640â42-38">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMasalha200732_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMasalha200732-39">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> speaks of a larger theologically-defined area, of which Palestine is a part, as the "land of Israel"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESaldarini199428â29_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESaldarini199428â29-40">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> (Î³á¿ á¼¸ÏÏÎ±Î®Î») (<a href="/wiki/Matthew_2:20%E2%80%9321" title="Matthew 2:20â21">Matthew 2:20â21</a>), in a narrative paralleling that of the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Exodus" title="Book of Exodus">Book of Exodus</a>.</span>

<li id="cite_note-42"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-42">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The parallels between this narrative and that of Exodus continue to be drawn. Like Pharaoh before him, Herod, having been frustrated in his original efforts, now seeks to achieve his objectives by implementing a program of infanticide. As a result, here â as in Exodus â rescuing the hero's life from the clutches of the evil king necessitates a sudden flight to another country. And finally, in perhaps the most vivid parallel of all, the present narrative uses virtually the same words of the earlier one to provide the information that the coast is clear for the herds safe return: here, in Matthew 2:20, 'go [back]â¦ for those who sought the child's life are dead; there, in Exodus 4:19, go backâ¦ for all the men who sought your life are dead'" (<a href="#CITEREFGoldberg2001">Goldberg 2001</a>, p.&#160;147).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-43">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Other writers, such as <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a>, referred to the region as <i><a href="/wiki/Coele-Syria" title="Coele-Syria">Coele-Syria</a></i> ("all Syria") around 10â20 CE (<a href="#CITEREFFeldman1996">Feldman 1996</a>, pp.&#160;557â558).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-47"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-47">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Several scholars hold the revisionist thesis that the Israelites did not move to the area as a distinct and foreign ethnic group at all, bringing with them their god Yahwe and forcibly evicting the indigenous population, but that they gradually evolved out of an amalgam of several ethnic groups, and that the Israelite cult developed on "Palestinian" soil amid the indigenous population. This would make the Israelites "Palestinians" not just in geographical and political terms (under the British Mandate, both Jews and Arabs living in the country were defined as Palestinians), but in ethnic and broader cultural terms as well. While this does not conform to the conventional view, or to the understanding of most Jews (and Arabs, for that matter), it is not easy to either prove or disprove. For although the Bible speaks at length about how the Israelites "took" the land, it is not a history book to draw reliable maps from. There is nothing in the extra-biblical sources, including the extensive Egyptian materials, to document the sojourn in Egypt or the exodus so vividly described in the Bible (and commonly dated to the thirteenth century). Biblical scholar Moshe Weinfeld sees the biblical account of the exodus, and of Moses and Joshua as founding heroes of the "national narration," as a later rendering of a lived experience that was subsequently either "forgotten" or consciously repressed â a textbook case of the "invented tradition" so familiar to modern students of ethnicity and nationalism." (<a href="#CITEREFKrÃ¤mer2011">KrÃ¤mer 2011</a>, p.&#160;8)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-50">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">(<a href="#CITEREFTemple_of_Jerusalem"><i>Temple of Jerusalem</i></a>): totally destroyed the building in 587/586</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"In both the Idumaean and the Ituraean alliances, and in the annexation of Samaria, the Judaeans had taken the leading role. They retained it. The whole politicalâmilitaryâreligious league that now united the hill country of Palestine from Dan to Beersheba, whatever it called itself, was directed by, and soon came to be called by others, 'the Ioudaioi'" (<a href="#CITEREFSmith1999">Smith 1999</a>, p.&#160;210a)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-69"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-69">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For example, in a 2011 review of the state of modern scholarship, <a href="/wiki/Bart_Ehrman" class="mw-redirect" title="Bart Ehrman">Bart Ehrman</a> (a secular agnostic) described the dispute, whilst concluding: "He certainly existed, as virtually every competent scholar of antiquity, Christian or non-Christian, agrees" (<a href="#CITEREFEhrman2011">Ehrman 2011</a>, p.&#160;285)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-83"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-83">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The religious situation also evolved under the new masters. Christianity did remain the majority religion, but it lost the privileges it had enjoyed." (<a href="#CITEREFFlusin2011">Flusin 2011</a>, pp.&#160;199â226, 215)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-85"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-85">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The earlier view, exemplifed by the writings of Moshe Gil, argued for a Jewish-Samaritan majority at the time of conquest: "We may reasonably state that at the time if the Muslim conquest, a large Jewish population still lived in Palestine. We do not know whether they formed the majority but we may assume with some certainly that they did so when grouped together with the Samaritans." (<a href="#CITEREFGil1997">Gil 1997</a>, p.&#160;3)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-91"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-91">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Under the Tulunids, Syro-Egyptian territory was deeply imbued with the concept of an extraordinary role devolving upon Jerusalem in Islam as al-Quds, Bayt al-Maqdis or Bayt al-Muqaddas, the "House of Holiness", the seat of the Last Judgment, the Gate to Paradise for Muslims as well as for Jews and Christians. In the popular conscience, this concept established a bond between the three monotheistic religions. If Ahmad ibn Tulun was interred on the slope of the <a href="/wiki/Mokattam" title="Mokattam">Muqattam</a> [near Cairo], <a href="/wiki/Isa_al-Nushari" title="Isa al-Nushari">Isa ibn Musa al-Nashari</a> and <a href="/wiki/Takin_al-Khazari" title="Takin al-Khazari">Takin</a> were laid to rest in Jerusalem in 910 and 933, as were their <a href="/wiki/Ikhshidid" class="mw-redirect" title="Ikhshidid">Ikhshidid</a> successors and <a href="/wiki/Abu_al-Misk_Kafur" title="Abu al-Misk Kafur">Kafir</a> [for context see <a href="/wiki/List_of_governors_of_Islamic_Egypt#Governors_during_the_Second_Abbasid_Period_(905â935)" class="mw-redirect" title="List of governors of Islamic Egypt">here</a>]. To honor the great general and governor of Syria <a href="/wiki/Anushtakin_al-Dizbari" title="Anushtakin al-Dizbari">Anushtakin al-Dizbiri</a>, who died in 433/1042, the <a href="/wiki/Fatimid_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Fatimid Dynasty">Fatimid Dynasty</a> had his remains solemnly conveyed from Aleppo to Jerusalem in 448/1056-57." (<a href="#CITEREFBianquis1998">Bianquis 1998</a>, p.&#160;103)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-118"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-118">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"In 1914 about 12,000 Jewish farmers and fieldworkers lived in approximately forty Jewish settlements&#160;â and to repeat it once again, they were by no means all Zionists. The dominant languages were still Yiddish, Russian, Polish, Rumanian, Hungarian, or German in the case of Ashkenazi immigrants from Europe, and Ladino (or 'Judeo-Spanish') and Arabic in the case of Sephardic and Oriental Jews. Biblical Hebrew served as the sacred language, while modern Hebrew (Ivrit) remained for the time being the language of a politically committed minority that had devoted itself to a revival of 'Hebrew culture'." (<a href="#CITEREFKrÃ¤mer2011">KrÃ¤mer 2011</a>, p.&#160;120)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-128"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-128">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Transjordan, however, controlled large portions of Judea and Samaria, later known as the West Bank" (<a href="#CITEREFTuckerRoberts2008">Tucker &amp; Roberts 2008</a>, pp.&#160;248â249, 500, 522)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-136"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-136">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The majority of the international community (including the UN General Assembly, the United Nations Security Council, the European Union, the International Criminal Court, and the vast majority of human rights organizations) considers Israel to be continuing to occupying Gaza, the West Bank and East Jerusalem. The government of Israel and some supporters have, at times, disputed this position of the international community. In 2011, Andrew Sanger explained the situation as follows: "Israel claims it no longer occupies the Gaza Strip, maintaining that it is neither a Stale nor a territory occupied or controlled by Israel, but rather it has 'sui generis' status. Pursuant to the Disengagement Plan, Israel dismantled all military institutions and settlements in Gaza and there is no longer a permanent Israeli military or civilian presence in the territory. However the Plan also provided that Israel will guard and monitor the external land perimeter of the Gaza Strip, will continue to maintain exclusive authority in Gaza air space, and will continue to exercise security activity in the sea off the coast of the Gaza Strip as well as maintaining an Israeli military presence on the Egyptian-Gaza border. and reserving the right to reenter Gaza at will. Israel continues to control six of Gaza's seven land crossings, its maritime borders and airspace and the movement of goods and persons in and out of the territory. Egypt controls one of Gaza's land crossings. Troops from the Israeli Defence Force regularly enter pans of the territory and/or deploy missile attacks, drones and sonic bombs into Gaza. Israel has declared a no-go buffer zone that stretches deep into Gaza: if Gazans enter this zone they are shot on sight. Gaza is also dependent on Israel for inter alia electricity, currency, telephone networks, issuing IDs, and permits to enter and leave the territory. Israel also has sole control of the Palestinian Population Registry through which the Israeli Army regulates who is classified as a Palestinian and who is a Gazan or West Banker. Since 2000 aside from a limited number of exceptions Israel has refused to add people to the Palestinian Population Registry. It is this direct external control over Gaza and indirect control over life within Gaza that has led the United Nations, the UN General Assembly, the UN Fact Finding Mission to Gaza, International human rights organisations, US Government websites, the UK Foreign and Commonwealth Office and a significant number of legal commentators, to reject the argument that Gaza is no longer occupied.",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanger2011429_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanger2011429-133">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> and in 2012 Iain Scobbie explained: "Even after the accession to power of Hamas, Israel's claim that it no longer occupies Gaza has not been accepted by UN bodies, most States, nor the majority of academic commentators because of its exclusive control of its border with Gaza and crossing points including the effective control it exerted over the Rafah crossing until at least May 2011, its control of Gaza's maritime zones and airspace which constitute what Aronson terms the 'security envelope' around Gaza, as well as its ability to intervene forcibly at will in Gaza"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScobbie2012295_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScobbie2012295-134">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> and Michelle Gawerc wrote in the same year: "While Israel withdrew from the immediate territory, Israel still controlled all access to and from Gaza through the border crossings, as well as through the coastline and the airspace. ln addition, Gaza was dependent upon Israel for water electricity sewage communication networks and for its trade (Gisha 2007. Dowty 2008). In other words, while Israel maintained that its occupation of Gaza ended with its unilateral disengagement Palestinians â as well as many human right organizations and international bodies â argued that Gaza was by all intents and purposes still occupied."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGawerc201244_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGawerc201244-135">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><br />For more details of this terminology dispute, including with respect to the current status of the Gaza Strip, see <a href="/wiki/Israeli-occupied_territories#International_views" title="Israeli-occupied territories">International views on the Israeli-occupied territories</a> and <a href="/wiki/Status_of_territories_captured_by_Israel" class="mw-redirect" title="Status of territories captured by Israel">Status of territories captured by Israel</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-144"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-144">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For an explanation of the differences between an annexed but disputed territory (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibet</a>) and a militarily occupied territory, please see the article <a href="/wiki/Military_occupation" title="Military occupation">Military occupation</a>. The "longest military occupation" description has been described in a number of ways, including: "The Israeli occupation of the West Bank and Gaza is the longest military occupation in modern times,"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHajjar200596_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHajjar200596-137">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> "...longest official military occupation of modern historyâcurrently entering its thirty-fifth year,"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson2001_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson2001-138">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> "...longest-lasting military occupation of the modern age, "<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMakdisi2010299_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMakdisi2010299-139">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> "This is probably the longest occupation in modern international relations, and it holds a central place in all literature on the law of belligerent occupation since the early 1970s,"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKretzmer2012885_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKretzmer2012885-140">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> "These are settlements and a military occupation that is the longest in the twentieth and twenty-first century, the longest formerly being the Japanese occupation of Korea from 1910 to 1945. So this is thirty-three years old [in 2000], pushing the record,"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESaid200333_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESaid200333-141">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> "Israel is the only modern state that has held territories under military occupation for over four decades."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexandrowicz2012_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexandrowicz2012-142">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> In 2014 Sharon Weill provided further context, writing: "Although the basic philosophy behind the law of military occupation is that it is a temporary situation modem occupations have well demonstrated that <i>rien ne dure comme le provisoire</i> A significant number of post-1945 occupations have lasted more than two decades such as the occupations of Namibia by South Africa and of East Timor by Indonesia as well as the ongoing occupations of Northern Cyprus by Turkey and of Western Sahara by Morocco. The Israeli occupation of the Palestinian territories, <u>which is the longest in all occupation's history</u> has already entered its fifth decade."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeill201422_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeill201422-143">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-146"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-146">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly_resolution_67/19" title="United Nations General Assembly resolution 67/19">United Nations General Assembly resolution 67/19</a> for further details</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-148"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-148">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to the Jewish Encyclopedia published between 1901 and 1906:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJewish_Encyclopedia1906_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJewish_Encyclopedia1906-147">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> "Palestine extends, from 31Â° to 33Â° 20' N. latitude. Its southwest point (at Raphia, Tell Rifaá¸¥, southwest of Gaza) is about 34Â° 15' E. longitude, and its northwest point (mouth of the Liá¹­ani) is at 35Â° 15' E. longitude, while the course of the Jordan reaches 35Â° 35' to the east. The west-Jordan country has, consequently, a length of about 150 English miles from north to south, and a breadth of about 23 miles (37&#160;km) at the north and 80 miles (129&#160;km) at the south. The area of this region, as measured by the surveyors of the English Palestine Exploration Fund, is about 6,040 square miles (15,644&#160;km<sup>2</sup>). The east-Jordan district is now being surveyed by the German PalÃ¤stina-Verein, and although the work is not yet completed, its area may be estimated at 4,000 square miles (10,360&#160;km<sup>2</sup>). This entire region, as stated above, was not occupied exclusively by the Israelites, for the plain along the coast in the south belonged to the Philistines, and that in the north to the Phoenicians, while in the east-Jordan country, the Israelitic possessions never extended farther than the Arnon (Wadi al-Mujib) in the south, nor did the Israelites ever settle in the most northerly and easterly portions of the plain of Bashan. To-day the number of inhabitants does not exceed 650,000. Palestine, and especially the Israelitic state, covered, therefore, a very small area, approximating that of the state of Vermont." From the Jewish Encyclopedia</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-150"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-150">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to the <a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica_Eleventh_Edition" title="EncyclopÃ¦dia Britannica Eleventh Edition">EncyclopÃ¦dia Britannica Eleventh Edition</a> (1911), Palestine is:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEB1911_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEB1911-149">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> "[A] geographical name of rather loose application. Etymological strictness would require it to denote exclusively the narrow strip of coast-land once occupied by the Philistines, from whose name it is derived. It is, however, conventionally used as a name for the territory which, in the Old Testament, is claimed as the inheritance of the pre-exilic Hebrews; thus it may be said generally to denote the southern third of the province of Syria. Except in the west, where the country is bordered by the Mediterranean Sea, the limit of this territory cannot be laid down on the map as a definite line. The modern subdivisions under the jurisdiction of the Ottoman Empire are in no sense conterminous with those of antiquity, and hence do not afford a boundary by which Palestine can be separated exactly from the rest of Syria in the north, or from the Sinaitic and Arabian deserts in the south and east; nor are the records of ancient boundaries sufficiently full and definite to make possible the complete demarcation of the country. Even the convention above referred to is inexact: it includes the Philistine territory, claimed but never settled by the Hebrews, and excludes the outlying parts of the large area claimed in Num. xxxiv. as the Hebrew possession (from the " River of Egypt " to Hamath). However, the Hebrews themselves have preserved, in the proverbial expression " from Dan to Beersheba " (Judg. xx.i, &amp;c.), an indication of the normal north-and-south limits of their land; and in defining the area of the country under discussion it is this indication which is generally followed. Taking as a guide the natural features most nearly corresponding to these outlying points, we may describe Palestine as the strip of land extending along the eastern shore of the Mediterranean Sea from the mouth of the Litany or Kasimiya River (33Â° 20' N.) southward to the mouth of the Wadi Ghuzza; the latter joins the sea in 31Â° 28' N., a short distance south of Gaza, and runs thence in a south-easterly direction so as to include on its northern side the site of Beersheba. Eastward there is no such definite border. The River Jordan, it is true, marks a line of <a href="/wiki/Delimitation" class="mw-redirect" title="Delimitation">delimitation</a> between Western and <a href="/wiki/Transjordan_(region)" title="Transjordan (region)">Eastern Palestine</a>; but it is practically impossible to say where the latter ends and the Arabian desert begins. Perhaps the line of the pilgrim road from Damascus to Mecca is the most convenient possible boundary. The total length of the region is about 140&#160;m (459.32&#160;ft); its breadth west of the Jordan ranges from about 23&#160;m (75.46&#160;ft) in the north to about 80&#160;m (262.47&#160;ft) in the south."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-163"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-163">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The term Palestine in the textbooks refers to Palestinian National Authority." (<a href="#CITEREFAdwan2006">Adwan 2006</a>, p.&#160;242)</span>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEJacobson1999&#91;&#91;Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_February_2021&#93;&#93;&lt;sup_class=&quot;noprint_Inline-Template_&quot;_style=&quot;white-space:nowrap;&quot;&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;&#91;&#91;Wikipedia:Citing_sources&#124;&lt;span_title=&quot;This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(February_2021)&quot;&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;&#93;&#93;&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJacobson1999[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_February_2021]]&lt;sup_class=&quot;noprint_Inline-Template_&quot;_style=&quot;white-space:nowrap;&quot;&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title=&quot;This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(February_2021)&quot;&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_23-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFJacobson1999">Jacobson 1999</a>, p.&#160;<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (February 2021)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>: "In the earliest Classical literature references to Palestine generally applied to the Land of Israel in the wider sense. A reappraisal of this question has given rise to the proposition that the name Palestine, in its Greek form Palaistine, was both a transliteration of a word used to describe the land of the Philistines and, at the same time, a literal translation of the name Israel. This dual interpretation reconciles apparent contradictions in early definitions of the name Palaistine and is compatible with the Greeks' penchant for punning, especially on place names."</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-Drews51-28"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Drews51_28-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFDrews1998">Drews 1998</a>, p.&#160;51: "The LXX's regular translation of ×¤Ö°Ö¼×Ö´×©Ö°××ªÖ´Ö¼×× into Î¬Î»Î»ÏÏÏÎ»Î¿Î¹ is significant here. If we assume, as I think we must, that with their word allophyloi the translators of the LXX tried to convey in Greek what p'liÅ¡tÃ®m had conveyed in Hebrew, we must conclude that for the worshippers of Yahweh p'liÅ¡tÃ®m and b'nÃª yiÅrÄ'Äl were mutually exclusive terms, p'liÅ¡tÃ®m (or allophyloi) being tantamount to 'non-Judaeans of the Promised Land' when used in a context of the third century BCE, and to 'non-Israelites of the Promised Land' when used in a context of Samson, Saul and David. <li id="cite_note-Risalesi-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Risalesi_35-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Risalesi_35-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFTamari2011">Tamari 2011</a>, pp.&#160;29â30: "Filastin Risalesi, is the salnameh type military handbook issued for Palestine at the beginning of the Great War... The first is a general map of the country in which the boundaries extend far beyond the frontiers of the Mutasarflik of Jerusalem, which was, until then, the standard delineation of Palestine. The northern borders of this map include the city of Tyre (Sur) and the Litani River, thus encompassing all of the Galilee and parts of southern Lebanon, as well as districts of Nablus, Haifa and Akkaâall of which were part of the Wilayat of Beirut until the end of the war."</span>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEWenning200726:_All_that_can_be_said_with_certainty_is_that_the_Nabataeans_are_known_in_the_sources_since_the_fourth_century_B.C._Up_to_that_time_the_Qedarites,_the_dominant_Arab_tribe_of_the_Persian_period,_controlled_the_south_from_the_Hejaz_and_all_of_the_Negev-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWenning200726:_All_that_can_be_said_with_certainty_is_that_the_Nabataeans_are_known_in_the_sources_since_the_fourth_century_B.C._Up_to_that_time_the_Qedarites,_the_dominant_Arab_tribe_of_the_Persian_period,_controlled_the_south_from_the_Hejaz_and_all_of_the_Negev_58-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFWenning2007">Wenning 2007</a>, pp.&#160;26: All that can be said with certainty is that the Nabataeans are known in the sources since the fourth century B.C. Up to that time the Qedarites, the dominant Arab tribe of the Persian period, controlled the south from the Hejaz and all of the Negev.</span>

<li id="cite_note-59"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-59">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">David F. Graf, 'Petra and the Nabataeans in the Early Hellenistic Period: the literary and archaeological evidence,' in Michel Mouton, Stephan G. Schmid (eds.), <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=bpWGAgAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA48"><i>Men on the Rocks: The Formation of Nabataean Petra</i></a>, Logos Verlag Berlin GmbH, 2013 pp.35â55 pp.47â48: 'the Idumean texts indicate that a large portion of the community in southern Palestine were Arabs, many of whom have names similar to those in the "Nabataean" onomasticon of later periods.' (p.47).</span>

<li id="cite_note-60"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-60">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Founded in the years 22-10 or 9 B.C. by Herod the Great, close to the ruins of a small Phoenician naval station named Strato's Tower (Stratonos Pyrgos, Turns Stratonis), which flourished during the 3d to 1st c. B.C. This small harbor was situated on the N part of the site. Herod dedicated the new town and its port (<i>limen Sebastos</i>) to <a href="/wiki/Caesar_Augustus" class="mw-redirect" title="Caesar Augustus">Caesar Augustus</a>. During the Early Roman period Caesarea was the seat of the Roman procurators of the province of Judea. Vespasian, proclaimed emperor at Caesarea, raised it to the rank of Colonia Prima Flavia Augusta, and later Alexander Severus raised it to the rank of Metropolis Provinciae Syriae Palestinae." A. Negev, "CAESAREA MARITIMA Palestine, Israel" in: Richard Stillwell et al. (eds.), <i>The Princeton Encyclopedia of Classical Sites</i> (1976).</span>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEEshel2008125:_Although_Dio&#39;s_figure_of_985_as_the_number_of_villages_destroyed_during_the_war_seems_hyperbolic,_all_Judaean_villages,_without_exception,_excavated_thus_far_were_razed_following_the_Bar_Kochba_Revolt._This_evidence_supports_the_impression_of_total_regional_destruction_following_the_war.-73"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEshel2008125:_Although_Dio&#39;s_figure_of_985_as_the_number_of_villages_destroyed_during_the_war_seems_hyperbolic,_all_Judaean_villages,_without_exception,_excavated_thus_far_were_razed_following_the_Bar_Kochba_Revolt._This_evidence_supports_the_impression_of_total_regional_destruction_following_the_war._73-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFEshel2008">Eshel 2008</a>, pp.&#160;125: Although Dio's figure of 985 as the number of villages destroyed during the war seems hyperbolic, all Judaean villages, without exception, excavated thus far were razed following the Bar Kochba Revolt. This evidence supports the impression of total regional destruction following the war..</span>

<li id="cite_note-H.H._Ben-Sasson,_1976,_page_334-75"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-H.H._Ben-Sasson,_1976,_page_334_75-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">H.H. Ben-Sasson, <i>A History of the Jewish People</i>, Harvard University Press, 1976, <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-674-39731-6" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-674-39731-6">978-0-674-39731-6</a>, page 334: "In an effort to wipe out all memory of the bond between the Jews and the land, Hadrian changed the name of the province from Iudaea to Syria-Palestina, a name that became common in non-Jewish literature."</span>
